<div id="review_3164446136" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3164446136" />
    <a title="Alain Dib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70886726-alain-dib"><img alt="Alain Dib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507354926p2/70886726.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3164446136?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alain Dib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alain Dib" href="/user/show/70886726-alain-dib">Alain Dib</a>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3164446136" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7411209404671415043">Existentialism is a humanism.<br/>It was a very simple and quick read. It isn't particularly complicated and it is a must read if you would like to get into existentialism.<br/>In fact, it's a conference Sartre gave to clarify what he means by the doctrine he names existentialism.<br/>Existentialism is a philosophical concept which affirms that existence preceeds essence.<br/>In short it means that man is what he makes of himself. Once he is part of the world he can be whatever he wants to be. Worse than that he ha</span>
  <span id="freeText7411209404671415043" style="display:none">Existentialism is a humanism.<br />It was a very simple and quick read. It isn't particularly complicated and it is a must read if you would like to get into existentialism.<br />In fact, it's a conference Sartre gave to clarify what he means by the doctrine he names existentialism.<br />Existentialism is a philosophical concept which affirms that existence preceeds essence.<br />In short it means that man is what he makes of himself. Once he is part of the world he can be whatever he wants to be. Worse than that he has to become something ultimately. Thus, man is condemned to be free for he cannot escape the reality that he can at any chosen time make choices that will gradually define him. For Sartre, refusing to make a choice is itself a choice. So when you don't chose you still technically do.<br />Also, Sartre consider that there is no God. Although there is a christian existentialism(Kierkegaard). So, he claims that there is no judgment of values because no one can decide except God and since there is no God it's up to us to decide what is best. <br />Existentialism is an attempt to make human beings accountable for their choices. Furthermore, what it is trying to say is that there is always a choice to be made. And, when you think that you didn't have a choice in fact you had one but you chose the other it's as simple as that.<br />Also when chosing according to Sartre you are redefining what makes man. For man isn't what he thinks he is but what he actually does. Which is why Sartre put so much accent on actions because when you watch a show or meet people they tell you what they actually do or what they did not what they thought and that their intention was such or other things.<br />Existentialism isn't a doctrine of despair holding us accountable for everything. On the contrary, it is a doctrine that tries to tell us that we are capable of so much more and that often the one who is making us miserable is us.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7411209404671415043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3164446136">

<div id="review_752095663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/752095663" />
    <a title="Abeer Abdullah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16996132-abeer-abdullah"><img alt="Abeer Abdullah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497810780p2/16996132.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/752095663?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abeer Abdullah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abeer Abdullah" href="/user/show/16996132-abeer-abdullah">Abeer Abdullah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780413313003" href="/book/show/822729.Existentialism_and_Humanism">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16996132-abeer-abdullah?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16996132-abeer-abdullah?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer752095663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8356951578373795983">I have a very skinny knowledge of western philosophy, I might be able to name important schools of thoughts, but will probably give you a very confused and primitive definition of each of them, so I decided to try and change that. <br/>the current meaning (or at at least my inference of the meaning via context) of the word 'existentialism' is something like contemplating your own existence. But Sartre's ideology is actually quite different, and much less pleasant (in my opinion anyway).<br/>in this 40 pag</span>
  <span id="freeText8356951578373795983" style="display:none">I have a very skinny knowledge of western philosophy, I might be able to name important schools of thoughts, but will probably give you a very confused and primitive definition of each of them, so I decided to try and change that. <br />the current meaning (or at at least my inference of the meaning via context) of the word 'existentialism' is something like contemplating your own existence. But Sartre's ideology is actually quite different, and much less pleasant (in my opinion anyway).<br />in this 40 page lecture (excluding the questioner in the end and the pretty lengthy introduction) Sartre tries to accurately convey the concept of existentialism and correct common misconceptions and most importantly defend it against the numerous backlash it got. people viewed it as a very pessimistic and cruel view. and cruel it may be but pessimistic it is not. it's probably the exact opposite.<br />the existentialist sees no evidence of the existence of god, and no clear cut signs for morality, and no universal human guidance and says that man is free. free to chose, the existentialist views the absence of god as disappointing and embarrassing and finds him self without excuses or guidance, the existentialist believes that man is condemned to be free, with the whole weight of responsibility of his own actions, in face the existentialist believes that when one chooses something for himself he choses it for the whole of mankind, and therefor man is always in anguish over the perplexity and responsibility.<br />(I just found a note I wrote next to the line which I found most interesting and poetic "man is condemned to be free" my note said "this freedom refers to loss, the loss of boundaries and by boundaries I think what is meant is guidance and justification, and that's completely true")<br />and now I'm going to mention things that I did not like about this specific ideology:<br />-the existentialist believes that man is born with his existence preceding his essence, meaning there is no human nature and man makes what he makes of him self "there is no human nature because there is no god to have a conception of it" and regardless of wether or not you believe in god human nature is a result of years of evolution of inherited genetic codings that do make a huge difference in a person's actions and preferences. <br />- that if man belongs to a certain religion then "registration is my will for everyone, and my action is, in consequence a commitment on behalf of all mankind" or if he were to get married then he is "thereby committing not only myself, but humanity as a whole, to the practice of monogamy" and I think this sounds ridiculous to a lot of people mostly because we are not a race that is ever truly sure of the correctness of our choices for the exact same reason that he himself noted before, we dont have any guidance, not a universal one at least. but that does not mean that we do not have a human nature, but human nature only serves as instincts, a disposition and desires. <br />and this attitude assumes a certain certainty that no honest man has and should definitely not prevail upon others.<br />-man is nothing else but the sum of his actions and what his life is "man commits himself, draws his own portrait, and there is nothing else but that portrait" although I whole heartedly believe the whole of the human race would evolve immensely in every aspect if we all followed the ideology that we are nothing but the sum of our actions and that our actions effect the whole of mankind I just dont agree with most of this. this assumes that man only exists in relation to his community. and that his existence is only what he gives and what he is remembered by and that is not only a terrible thing to hear but is simply untrue. for one thing there is no concrete evidence that everyone could uphold that proves the specific importance of being remembered or contributing to your society. but the pursuit of joy and happiness and even momentary pleasure seems to be pretty universal. I really dont want to be a part of a human society that only exists to make and achieve more, that is probably an even more miserable one. man exists within himself and his experiences matter very much if only to himself but most importantly to him self, and I don't know, loving somebody is much more important (to me and to a lot of people and that is the point, understanding human variety) than drawing your portrait or the sum of your actions.<br />-another thing that seems to be implied, and I don't know if i misunderstood this, is the complete denial of the effect of heredity and life experiences (nature AND nurture, wow) on a person's actions and only cowards and 'self deceivers' take those as an excuse to not do something. and that the human will is everything.<br />well, I dont know, yes the human will could maybe overcome both nature and nurture, it has in the past, but it's just very unfair to completely dismiss someone's difficulties and equate them with someone perfectly gifted and privileged. people are complex, undeniable combinations of nature, nurture AND will power. and its unfair, ignorant, and cruel to assume that everyone has the same chances to preform a certain task. <br /><br />lastly, existentialism is a very interesting and accomplished ideology that offers many great realistic and optimistic points, like this personal favorite "if, however, it means that whatever man may now appear to be, there is a future to be fashioned, a virgin future that awaits him---then it is a true saying. but in the present one is forsaken"<br />and some fairly noble pursuits that understands the humility of mankind and has pretty big hopes for it. but I think maybe it truly underestimated the great and undeniable power of feelings. <br /> (skip to page 66 for relation between existentialism and humanism) </span>
  <a data-text-id="8356951578373795983" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Golshan Tabatabaie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Golshan Tabatabaie" href="/user/show/53047530-golshan-tabatabaie">Golshan Tabatabaie</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2886332010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8784992305539438629"><b> Existentialism is humanism because it reminds man that there is no legislator but himself; that he himself, thus abandoned, must decide for himself. </b><br/><br/>To put it briefly, Jean-Paul Satre explains existentialism better than anybody else. You think you know what it is and then you read his work and you'll get reminded of its other aspects and you fall for it even more!<br/><blockquote> Life is nothing until it is lived; but it is yours to make sense of, and the value of it is nothing else but the sense that you choo</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText8784992305539438629" style="display:none"><b> Existentialism is humanism because it reminds man that there is no legislator but himself; that he himself, thus abandoned, must decide for himself. </b><br /><br />To put it briefly, Jean-Paul Satre explains existentialism better than anybody else. You think you know what it is and then you read his work and you'll get reminded of its other aspects and you fall for it even more!<br /><blockquote> Life is nothing until it is lived; but it is yours to make sense of, and the value of it is nothing else but the sense that you choose. </blockquote><br />Existentialism is not at all what people assume it is. It isn't despair. How can it be all anguish when it shows you that you yourself are the centre of your life. Everything is up to you.<br /><blockquote> It can not be regarded as a philosophy of quietism since it defines man by his action. </blockquote><br />The best possible choice of reading after this book has got to be Existential Psychotherapy by Irvin D. Yalom.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1009250384" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4987278342865745460">It is the rosebud of sartre. A conference given in front of a hétérogenous people which he wants to seduce. An assembly very hype like at a seminars of Lacan. <br/>There is a certain imposture has to associate humanism and existentialism. This last is naturally individualistic whereas the humanism is a collective project. Manages he to bring closer these two terms. I do not believe. And there is already this obsession to sermonizer. Sartre takes for example the german occupation and the choice of Res</span>
  <span id="freeText4987278342865745460" style="display:none">It is the rosebud of sartre. A conference given in front of a hétérogenous people which he wants to seduce. An assembly very hype like at a seminars of Lacan. <br />There is a certain imposture has to associate humanism and existentialism. This last is naturally individualistic whereas the humanism is a collective project. Manages he to bring closer these two terms. I do not believe. And there is already this obsession to sermonizer. Sartre takes for example the german occupation and the choice of Resistance. He allots the epithet of coward and bastards. However Sartre did not suffer from occupation. His play "The flys" was presented in 1943 with agreement of the German censure.<br />Curiosly, Sartre did everything so that this text wass occulted. When I was at high-school, we could get it only at Swiss Nagel editor without agreement of Sartre. The version proposed here carefully is re-examined and commented on by his daughter.<br />At least, he often quotes Heidegger. In fact the great regret of Sartre is not to be Heidegger. That with undoubtedly thorough with all dogmatic excesses which discredited him. The greatest philosopher of after war is Heidegger.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2396928321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12688777320365353327">After quite a long time I am giving a book 5 stars for Sartre's outstanding power of reasoning over and illustrating the various aspects of existentialism against the charges that have been brought against it. I am usually not the kind of person who always scribbles down the major points in her diary from the books she reads, but regarding this one, I had to.<br/>(Nothing to say of the cover of the book, it perfectly suits.)</span>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer8658103408544247904">A good introduction to Sartre's type of existentialism. I almost entirely disagree with his perspective but it is interesting reading anyway. M.Naville's opening arguments in the 'discussion' section is worth a read too.</span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2344237461">
  <td colspan="2">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2886923962" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2886923962" />
    <a title="Xander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60074558-xander"><img alt="Xander" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490713726p2/60074558.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2886923962?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Xander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Xander" href="/user/show/60074558-xander">Xander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2886923962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6848004496116040902">This book is sold for (literally) 12 euro's on the internet, but is mostly stuffed with filler. The part which probably concerns readers most - Jean-Paul Sartre's defense of his existentialist philosophy against marxist, christian and humanist critics - spans only 35 pages. The introduction to this essay is almost half the length of Sartre's work, which itself is followed by a Q&A written down and Sartre's essay on Albert Camus' novel L'Étranger (1942). So unfortunately I can't really rate this </span>
  <span id="freeText6848004496116040902" style="display:none">This book is sold for (literally) 12 euro's on the internet, but is mostly stuffed with filler. The part which probably concerns readers most - Jean-Paul Sartre's defense of his existentialist philosophy against marxist, christian and humanist critics - spans only 35 pages. The introduction to this essay is almost half the length of Sartre's work, which itself is followed by a Q&amp;A written down and Sartre's essay on Albert Camus' novel L'Étranger (1942). So unfortunately I can't really rate this work very highly - it is simply too little value for your money.<br /><br />Anyway, in the main essay Sartre tries to defend his existentialist philosophy - as worked out in his novels La Nausée (1938) and Le Mur (1939) as well as his philosophical treatise L'Être et le Neant (1943). Basically, existentialism is the worldview that the human condition is a starting point for any objectivity and morality - neither god nor a universal ethic can function as fundamental principle for human existence. And this human condition only exists in relation to other human existences; we exist for ourselves and for others, while others exist for us and themselves. Human beings are thus free to choose and with these choices are able to design their own life - but this doesn't lead to radical egotism but rather to an intersubjective existence. <br /><br />Three terms are crucial in this regard: anguish, abandonment and despair. <br /><br />1. We live in anguish because we have to make fundamental choices that shape our lives - me designing my own life is what constitutes human existence. The anguish arises because in choosing to live a particular life I am not just the designer of my own life but also legislating for humanity as such. In deciding to be X I judge that humanity should be X. So I'm not just responsible for my own life, which already is weighing down on me heavily, but also for humanity as such. And the anguish I feel in light of this fact, is what characterizes the human condition. There are many people who hide this anguish from themselves and from others, but they are merely acting in bad faith - 'mauvaise foi'. <br /><br />Sartre seems to aim his critique particularly at religious people and at determinists. Religious people retreat to themselves and exclaim "I behave thus because I want to behave thus, many others don't" while determinists exclaim "Everyone does not act thus". The religious person perceives himself as a subjective island, only responsible for himself in front of god; the determinist seeks universal values to base his behaviour on. Both are wrong: the human condition is the basis of human existence and freedom exists only intersubjectively. <br /><br />2. We are abandoned because we don't believe in god anymore. And with the death of god comes the breakdown of the fundamental principle of morals. Theologians and secularists who still look to rescue the former universal values from the rubble are doomed to fail. Sartre seems to see in Dostoyevski's phrase "Without God anything is permitted" the vindication of his own philosophy - the only difference being Sartre's conception of individual freedom as intimately connected to the freedom of other humans. I am free to choose my own life. In this I can choose anything I like, as long as my particular existence can be willed by all other human beings. <br /><br />To put it slightly popular: to avoid falling in the Dostoyevskian existential nihilism Sartre tries to apply Kant's universal principle without claiming that it's universal - just a principle. There is some truth in what Sartre claims: no universal moral code has ever been able to translate into particular instances. So when Kant claims that we should will that which can be willed universally without contradiction, this doesn't prescribe what an individual in his particular situation has to do. And the same goes for religious moral codes and utilitarianism as well. <br /><br />Sartre's solution is to place the individual and his situation at the centre of things: just be who you want to be, and in this project your version of humanity. But still, doesn't Sartre's condition still claims to be universal? Somehwere in his text he gives the following example. A lady falls in love with a man who is already married to a plain girl. Should the lady pursue her own passions and break up the marriage? Or should she decide to curb her passions to let them be? For Sartre both decisions are equivalent and both are the best option if the lady wills the one or the other. But how does his freedom-condition fit into this? Didn't he claim earlier that we are free to choose on the condition that our freedom can be willed by all of humanity? But this leads us back to the Kantian trap: Can I will that all of humanity wills to pursue their sexual lusts and make marriages impossible if what I want is a decent marriage? As far as I can tell, Sartre doesn't answer this problem - or perhaps I am misreading him. <br /><br />3. The third crucial term for Sartre is despair. This is despair. Although the word seems to be self-evident, Sartre means a particular thing with it: my actions are finite, have limits. We can will and hence do things but we ourselves operate in an objective world of probabilities. This means that there is a gradual shift from my action and subsequent resonsibility to objective probabilities. <br /><br />He mentions the example of picking up a friend at the train station. One assumes the friend actually took the train, the train started its journey at the appropriate time, wasn't derailed on its journey, etc. The right way to approach this situation is to focus on what is directly linked to myself, to recognize and acknowledge my limits and be indffierent to all the probabilities which are not encompassed by my actions. So I go to the station as planned and I will not busy myself with the multiplicity of external probabilities that would mean my friend does or does not show up, on time or not on time.<br /><br />Especially Sartre's use of despair led to the criticism that he preached a corrosive quietism - just accept all the infinite probabilities that are out of your control and resign yourself to this state of affairs beyond your control. But, or so Sartre claims, this is actually reversing the whole thing. Quietism is cowardism; existentialism emphasizes the choices that are actually within our control and hence we must make. So the masses of people who hide behind bad luck or deterministic excuses ("I was born this way") are cowards who try to escape their responsibilities. <br /><br />In general, Sartre seems to develop a very basal philosophical system: Descartes' "I think therefore I am" is the starting point and from this springs our subjective freedom. Man is nothing and is only through choosing, through being free. And it is in this Cartesian subjectivism that Sartre claims the humanism of his existentialist philosophy lies: he takes human existence as pivot of all meaning ("without man no meaning") emphasizes human responsibility to choose wisely and through this designing their own life (very anti-authoritarian), and sees inter-subjectivity as a crucial guideline for human freedom (we are free in relation to other free human beings and vice versa). My awareness, my realization of my own freedom confronts me with other human beings who are also conscious of their own freedom, and this situation forms that breeding ground, so to speak, for human action.<br /><br />Sartre wanted to defend himself against communists, christians and humanists. Communists claim that materialism determines human subjectivity, while Sartre views human subjectivity as the fundamental principle of all meaning. Also, communists claim that the world is determined by causal laws (historical or otherwise), while Sartre rejects causality and seems to have a radical conception of free will. At the same time, religious people hide from their own funademental freedom and that act in bad faith. They also claim universal values and universal facts, while Sartre denies all this and sees in the freedom to choose the core of all morality and objectivity. In this he draws heavily upon both Husserl and Heidegger - for Husserl human existence is the foundation for the objective world and we can only get to know this through studying our psychological make-up in a basic, non-interpretative way (i.e. phenomenology); for Heidegger human existence is all there is, and we (literally) are only in so far as we live in fear of death (i.e. have time as our existential horizon). And finally, the humanists claimed that Sartre was anti-humanist, since he seems to reject all situational influences on human existence and reduce a human being to some sort of choosing apparatus. <br /><br />Personally, I think the last critique is the most difficult to deal with for Sartre, since he indeed denies any determinist/causal mechanism. We are thrown into the world (vide Heidegger) and from that moment are forced to chooise and through this design (vide Heidegger) our own life. We are responsible for who we are, since we are the ones who chose to be this human being and not some other. Such an attitude would fit neatly in Sartre's own time (in which many people threw away their responsibility and turned either fascist or fascist-collaborator) as well as in any book on self-help, not to mention the military. But such an attitude seems quite un-philosophical for the simple fact that he doesn't adresses (1) how human existence, as a fundamental starting point of everything comes to be in the world; and (2) the knowledge in fields such as psychology and sociology which tells us that human beings are not only psychological, but mostly biological creatures. <br /><br />The problem is rather insurmountable. When Sartre exclaims "existence precedes essence" and with this denies the existence of any human nature - there is only the human condition, which forces every individual to design his own essence - he completely ignores the fact that we didn't choose our parents, the environment in our mother's womb, our upbringing during the first years, etc. etc. When does the freedom kick in? At the moment of conception? At birth? But then how does a baby deliberately design his own life? Or at some later moment? But then when? Puberty? But then we enter a phase of radical transformation which seems to hinder our freedom rather much... <br /><br />Basically, Sartre falls into the same trap that Descartes already fell in in the early seventeenth century: once your postulate the human psyche as the starting point of the world, you have to offer a plausible connection to the material world (including our own bodies). You can't have your cake and eat it. Claiming (literally) that human beings are what they want to be seems quite absurd to me. I can see how such a perspective on life is very healthy in a time when a World War is raging, during an occupation in which people seem to lose all sense of responsibility, and during the years of re-building your country and trying to avoid a resurgence of fascism. But one has to remember that Sartre wasn't a political writer, he explicitly puts forward a philosophical system, which (to me, at least) seems highly un-philosophical. <br /><br />I'm not really impressed by the short description Sartre gives of his existentialism in L'Existentialism est un Humanism (1945), but perhaps I will chance my mind or reconsider my positions after reading his magnum opus L'Être et le Neant....</span>
  <a data-text-id="6848004496116040902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3314221337" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3314221337" />
    <a title="André" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7215128-andr"><img alt="André" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586543083p2/7215128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3314221337?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="André" class="user" itemprop="url" name="André" href="/user/show/7215128-andr">André</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25735018-existentialism-is-a-humanism">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3314221337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10920339225246756792">Jean-Paul Sartre's work succinctly highlights the Existentialist thought. The work, though being challenging, presents topics about the self, consciousness, freedom, and the nature of meaning.<br/><br/>The first part of the book addresses the main definition of existentialism. On that point, Sartre claims that "Existence precedes essence". This means that things first exist and, then, they are given a certain value. The value, through our consciousness, determines a specific meaning. Thus, the value of a </span>
  <span id="freeText10920339225246756792" style="display:none">Jean-Paul Sartre's work succinctly highlights the Existentialist thought. The work, though being challenging, presents topics about the self, consciousness, freedom, and the nature of meaning.<br /><br />The first part of the book addresses the main definition of existentialism. On that point, Sartre claims that "Existence precedes essence". This means that things first exist and, then, they are given a certain value. The value, through our consciousness, determines a specific meaning. Thus, the value of a certain object must be created by the individual. For instance, a certain work of art has its intrinsic value according to society standards and the subjective level of an individual. However, in the artist's mind, that work of art was first crafted in his mind, but the final product was not yet conceived. For that reason, it was the artist's creativity that made it possible.<br />In contrast to that argument, linguistics can support that "Existence precedes essence" claim. In a way or another, languages have their systems, their sounds, and intonations. For instance, if a Spanish speaker (unaware of any other foreign language) hears a Korean orator, he won't be able to understand what the other person has to say. The Spanish speaker, as though aware of the existence of a language being spoken, has no idea WHAT language is being spoken and its real meaning (essence).<br /><br />"Man is nothing else but that which he makes of himself."<br />If "God is dead" and everything is permitted, everyone should take responsibility for their actions. However, in certain situations, our actions are driven by impulsive decisions that sometimes we can't control, at least at a consciousness level. In that case, that sense of responsibility can become a burden. If we are free (theologically speaking), we end up in a wider range of outcomes throughout our lives. For instance, most of us can make decisions. However, some actions are influenced by other means - such as peer-to-peer pressure, irrational decisions, impulsive conclusions, etc. Nonetheless, what about of those who are intellectually incapable of taking decisions on their own? Don't our surroundings indirectly influence our decisions, unconsciously? Life is an abundance of interconnected events that sometimes we can't predict...<br /><br />Throughout the book, it's defined the concepts of anguish, despair, and abandonment. If there's no god, anguish makes responsibility much heavier. On the other hand, despair is related to an individual's actions and their impact on mankind. Also, abandonment, the most interesting concept of Sartre's narrative, says that since we are free (theologically speaking), we are abandoned in the universe. The lonely feeling of atheism can lead to a certain degree of neuroticism and nihilism. If there's no God, in certain individuals, the feeling of abandonment can be quite overwhelming from an existential perspective. Nonetheless, since most of us can give value to things, our existential purpose becomes personal, subjective, and singular. Thus, in metaphysical lenses, that singularity can also be quite inspiring and liberating.<br /><br />Sartre's work proposes an interesting discussion regarding metaphysical problems, especially on Being and natural meaning. In my opinion, the metaphorical connotation of the word "Essence" delivers a powerful sense, a sense of personal significance, and value. Nevertheless, value and meaning might have different meanings to each one of us.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10920339225246756792" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Dany" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92136859-dany"><img alt="Dany" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564978342p2/92136859.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<span id="freeTextContainer10700843748884492192">“Certainly, many people think that in what they are doing they commit no one but themselves to anything: and if you ask them, “What would happen if everyone did so?” they shrug their shoulders and reply, “Everyone does not do so.” But in truth, one ought always to ask oneself what would happen if everyone did as one is doing; nor can one escape from that disturbing thought except by a kind of self-deception. The man who lies in self-excuse, by saying “Everyone will not do it” must be ill at ease</span>
  <span id="freeText10700843748884492192" style="display:none">“Certainly, many people think that in what they are doing they commit no one but themselves to anything: and if you ask them, “What would happen if everyone did so?” they shrug their shoulders and reply, “Everyone does not do so.” But in truth, one ought always to ask oneself what would happen if everyone did as one is doing; nor can one escape from that disturbing thought except by a kind of self-deception. The man who lies in self-excuse, by saying “Everyone will not do it” must be ill at ease in his conscience, for the act of lying implies the universal value which it denies. By its very disguise his anguish reveals itself. This is the anguish that Kierkegaard called “the anguish of Abraham.” “</span>

<div id="review_482972987" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/482972987" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/482972987?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=french-existentialists">french-existentialists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer482972987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2220423880775984750">I've been revisiting a lot of the writers I worshiped as an adolescent, Sartre among them... Sartre presents his case in Existentialism Is a Humanism with absolute clarity and purpose-- a major departure from Being and Nothingness. His main goal is to prove that existentialism is a humanism. On this front I suppose he succeeds.<br/><br/>But as far as building a case for why <u>his</u> existential perspective is a necessity, he starts to falter. I would most certainly agree that existence precedes essence. But fr</span>
  <span id="freeText2220423880775984750" style="display:none">I've been revisiting a lot of the writers I worshiped as an adolescent, Sartre among them... Sartre presents his case in Existentialism Is a Humanism with absolute clarity and purpose-- a major departure from Being and Nothingness. His main goal is to prove that existentialism is a humanism. On this front I suppose he succeeds.<br /><br />But as far as building a case for why <u>his</u> existential perspective is a necessity, he starts to falter. I would most certainly agree that existence precedes essence. But from that logic, Sartre depicts man as an isolated, atomized creature. He fails to account for existence in terms of his material conditions (qua Marx), his existence among various social forces (qua Veblen), his existence as an organism (qua Darwin), his existence as a being-in-the-world (qua Heidegger), or his existence as a set of subconscious drives (qua Freud). This isn't to say that any of these writers were always or even most-of-the-time right, but it is to say that Sartre's perspective, by cloaking itself in faux-revolutionary language and seeming to ignore all of these major insights, offers a frankly rather simplistic and doctrinaire philosophy. These are insights he addresses in Being and Nothingness, but Existentialism Is a Humanism is a thin and fairly flimsy treatise.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2220423880775984750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16764559254980955916">Broken down into two sections. The first section is the talk given by Sartre, where he describes existentialism in a sure-footed manner, referring to anguish, abandonment, and despair. But the book is worth reading for the second section, in which Sartre presents an analysis of Camus's The Stranger. His take on the novel is fascinating, and his analogies come in quite handy. This makes me want to revisit The Stranger in the near future.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7WFlg+a2dfYfTv2HLrNvZDebg4UGbXKvo8b/hmiWiyO1O7SuikJ07QCzWlUyxLqzvUhMSWOQto757S+IkPVIuw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7MUrZBq6TnGqXVAvrYtVcYA0w4vHApRUOMBw6WBp/cK0n/pJdk5ParWg9/2x/ICmCucMR6L/UHVi66DnmAo+Wg==')})
  }

It begins with a Preface, created after the rest of the manuscript was completed, that explains the core of his method and what sets it apart from any preceding philosophy. The Introduction, written before the rest of the work, summarizes and completes Kant\'s ideas on skepticism by rendering it moot and encouraging idealism and self-realization. The body of the work is divided into six sections of varying length, entitled &quot;Consciousness,&quot; &quot;Self-Consciousness,&quot; &quot;Reason,&quot; &quot;Spirit,&quot; &quot;Religion,&quot; and &quot;Absolute Knowledge.&quot; A myriad of topics are discussed, and explained in such a harmoniously complex way that the method has been termed Hegelian dialectic. Ultimately, the work as a whole is a remarkable study of the mind\'s growth from its direct awareness to scientific philosophy, proving to be a difficult yet highly influential and enduring Sobre todo en la actualidad se juzga y pondera su obra. no menos que su personalidad, de manera diferente. Para algunos, Descartes es de preferencia un metodólogo (W. Windelband, P. Natorp...) . Su preocupación, su gran preocupación consistió, según ellos, en dar un fundamento lógico a la nueva ciencia natural, como él mismo lo intentó y lo hizo. Descartes es, de cierto, así un clásico en la historia de la filosofía como en clásico en la historia de la ciencia. Para otros, la intención acuciante e íntima de Descartes era de orden moral y religioso (L. Blanchet, por ejemplo) : apaciguar el conflicto entre revelación y razón, entre fe y saber. De ahí, se dice, la importancia concedida a la idea de Dios en todo el sistema.\n\nUn tercer grupo enfatiza en las apreciaciones los perfiles ontológicos y metafísicos de la obra cartesiana: la finalidad reside, a su juicio, en vivir experiencias ontológicas del yo y del mundo (F. Nietzsche rewrites the former as a history of cruelty, exposing the central values of the Judaeo-Christian and liberal traditions - compassion, equality, justice - as the product of a brutal process of conditioning designed to domesticate the animal vitality of earlier cultures. The result is a book which raises profoundly disquieting issues about the violence of both ethics and interpretation. Nietzsche questions moral certainties by showing that religion and science have no claim to absolute truth, before turning on his own arguments in order to call their very presuppositions into question. The Genealogy is the most sustained of Nietzsche\'s later works and offers one of the fullest expressions of his characteristic concerns. This edition places his ideas within the cultural context of his own time and stresses the relevance of his work for a contemporary audience.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17414173390862490530\" During the conversation, other questions are raised: wh<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2914833999526543459\" style=\"display:none\">Presented in the form of a dialogue between Socrates and three different interlocutors, this classic text is an enquiry into the notion of a perfect community and the ideal individual within it. The Republic also addresses the purpose of education and the role of both women and men as guardians of the people. He is regarded as a leading pioneer of existentialism and one of the greatest philosophers of the 19th Century.\n\nIn Fear and Trembling, Kierkegaard wanted to understand the anxiety that must have been present in Abraham when God commanded him to offer his son as a human sacrifice. Abraham had a choice to complete the task or to forget it. He resigned himself to the loss of his son, acting according to his faith. In other words, one must be willing to give up all his or her earthly possessions in infinite resignation and must also be willing to give up whatever it is that he or she loves more than God. Abraham had passed the test -- his love for God proved greater than anything else in him. And because a good and just Creator would not want a father to kill his son, God intervened at the last moment to prevent the sacrifice.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"557772852903135988\" href=\"#\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3816936078985758137"><b>Jean-Paul Charles Aymard Sartre</b>, normally known simply as Jean-Paul Sartre, was a French existentialist philosopher and pioneer, dramatist and screenwriter, novelist and critic. He was a leading figure in 20th century French philosophy.<br/><br/>He declined the award of the 1964 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his work which, rich in ideas and filled with the spirit of freedom and the quest for truth, has ex</span>
  <span id="freeText3816936078985758137" style="display:none"><b>Jean-Paul Charles Aymard Sartre</b>, normally known simply as Jean-Paul Sartre, was a French existentialist philosopher and pioneer, dramatist and screenwriter, novelist and critic. He was a leading figure in 20th century French philosophy.<br /><br />He declined the award of the 1964 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his work which, rich in ideas and filled with the spirit of freedom and the quest for truth, has exerted a far-reaching influence on our age."<br /><br />In the years around the time of his death, however, existentialism declined in French philosophy and was overtaken by structuralism, represented by Levi-Strauss and, one of Sartre's detractors, Michel Foucault.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3816936078985758137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2376452-l-existentialisme-est-un-humanisme">Quotes from Existentialism An...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">

              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/243020-il-n-y-a-de-r-alit-que-dans-l-action-there-is">261 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;In life man commits himself and draws his own portrait, outside of which there is nothing. No doubt this thought may seem harsh to someone who has not made a success of his life. But on the other hand, it helps people to understand that reality alone counts, and that dreams, expectations and hopes only serve to define a man as a broken dream, aborted hopes, and futile expectations.&rdquo;</span>
